<h3>2. Hypothesis</h3>
<div id="interaction-between-mouse-type-and-treatment" class="section level5">
<h5>Interaction between mouse type and treatment</h5>
<p>We may see significantly different means between different combinations of mouse and treatment</p>

<div id="how-we-got-there" class="section level3">
<h3>3. How we got there?</h3>
<div id="maybe-number-of-spines-on-the-photo-means-something" class="section level4">
<h4>Maybe number of spines on the photo means something?</h4>
<p>As we may observe, it doesn’t really matter</p>
<pre class="r"><code>spines[, noOfSpines := max(spine_number), by = 'Animal:Photo']
model &lt;- lmer(length ~ treatment*mouse + (1|Animal) + (1|Photo_ID_abs), data = spines)
model_2 &lt;- lmer(length ~ treatment*mouse + (1|Animal) + (1|Photo_ID_abs) + (1|noOfSpines), data = spines)
anova(model, model_2)</code></pre>
<pre><code>## Data: spines
## Models:
## model: length ~ treatment * mouse + (1 | Animal) + (1 | Photo_ID_abs)
## model_2: length ~ treatment * mouse + (1 | Animal) + (1 | Photo_ID_abs) +
## model_2: (1 | noOfSpines)
## Df AIC BIC logLik deviance Chisq Chi Df Pr(&gt;Chisq)
## model 7 -2829.3 -2781.8 1421.7 -2843.3
## model_2 8 -2827.3 -2773.1 1421.7 -2843.3 2e-04 1 0.9892</code></pre>
</div>
<div id="fixed-effect-shall-be-mouse-and-treatment" class="section level4">
<h4>Fixed effect shall be mouse and treatment</h4>
<p>It is pretty straightforward from a logical point of view. Finding mouse type and treatment of a particular mouse is obvious (so it’s cheap) and there is comparatively small number of possible levels</p>
</div>
<div id="random-effects-shall-be-animal-and-photo" class="section level4">
<h4>Random effects shall be Animal and Photo</h4>
<p>Effect of a single animal is random - every study and every year animals are just different individuals. After a quick look at the data we may see that number of photos per animal is irregular. Interestingly, for our study (i.e. KO) there is no such case that particular photo number occurs for two (or more) different animals.</p>
</div>
<div id="why-photo-should-be-considered-as-nested-in-animal" class="section level4">
<h4>Why Photo should be considered as nested in Animal?</h4>
<p>Despite the fact it shouldn’t matter in our study (as stated just before) it is reasonable (and confirmed by simulations) to look at photos as ‘effects of single animal’.</p>
</div>
<div id="so-the-model-will-be-similar-to-length-treatment-mouse-1animalphoto" class="section level4">
<h4>So the model will be similar to length ~ treatment + mouse + (1|Animal/Photo)</h4>
<pre class="r"><code>modelZagniezdzony &lt;- lmer(length ~ mouse * treatment+ (1|Animal/Photo_ID_abs), data = spines)
qqnorm(residuals(modelZagniezdzony),pch=1)</code></pre>

title alt width="672" /></p>
<p>Unfortunately the assumptions of mixed models are not met, because residuals don’t have normal distribution. Looking at tail of this qqplot we guessed, that taking the logarithm of our dependant variable might be helpful.</p>
<p>We checked qqplots of many different models in logtrans and BoxCox class. After that we decided, that our model will be</p>
<pre class="r"><code>superModel &lt;- lmer(log(length-0.03) ~ treatment*mouse + (1|Animal/Photo_ID_abs),
data = spines)
summary(superModel)</code></pre>
<pre><code>## Linear mixed model fit by REML ['lmerMod']

## Data: spines
##

## Min 1Q Median 3Q Max
## -2.9411 -0.6623 0.0039 0.6463 3.3577
##

## Groups Name Variance Std.Dev.
## Photo_ID_abs:Animal (Intercept) 0.004616 0.06794
## Animal (Intercept) 0.004772 0.06908
## Residual 0.239649 0.48954
## Number of obs: 6512, groups: Photo_ID_abs:Animal, 65; Animal, 14

## (Intr) trtmnt mousWT

<pre class="r"><code>par(mfrow = c(1,1))
qqnorm(residuals(superModel))
qqline(residuals(superModel))</code></pre>

<p>Now we can assume, that the residuals follow the normal distribution. Another assumption is that u is normally distributed too. Let’s see whether it’s false or not.</p>
<pre class="r"><code>library(lattice)
u=ranef(superModel,postVar=T)
dotplot(u)</code></pre>

title alt width="672" /></p>
<p>Although middpoints of confidence intervals do not look exactly like normal cdf, we still can fit the theoretical normal cdf into our confidence intervals, therefore we don’t reject the hypothesis, that u has normal distribution.</p>
</div>
</div>
<div id="are-all-components-of-our-model-significant" class="section level3">
<h3>Are all components of our model significant?</h3>
<p>Firstly let’s check whether fixed effects are important by looking at p-values.</p>
<pre class="r"><code>tStat &lt;- summary(superModel)$coeff[,3]
pValues &lt;- sapply(1:length(tStat),function(i) {2*pnorm(abs(tStat[i]),lower.tail = F)})
pValues</code></pre>
<pre><code>## (Intercept) treatmentli mouseWT
## 3.699272e-140 2.542565e-01 1.711802e-04
## treatmentli:mouseWT
## 1.430942e-02</code></pre>
<p>This p-values give the same information as previously showed boxplots, that is intercept, mouse and interaction effects are significant, while treatment effect is not.</p>
<p>Should we include random effect? Yes, we should. It can be proved with permutational test</p>
<pre class="r"><code>N &lt;- 1000
logs &lt;- replicate(N, logLik(lmer(log(length-0.03) ~
treatment + mouse + sample(treatment:mouse) + (1|Animal/Photo_ID_abs), data = spines)))
mean(logs &gt; logLik(superModel))</code></pre>
<pre><code>## [1] 0</code></pre>
<p>We can check whether nested model is better then the simpler one by using ANOVA test.</p>
<pre class="r"><code>superModel2 &lt;- lmer(log(length-0.03) ~ treatment*mouse + (1|Animal),
data = spines)
anova(superModel2, superModel)</code></pre>
<pre><code>## Data: spines
## Models:
## superModel2: log(length - 0.03) ~ treatment * mouse + (1 | Animal)
## superModel: log(length - 0.03) ~ treatment * mouse + (1 | Animal/Photo_ID_abs)
## Df AIC BIC logLik deviance Chisq Chi Df Pr(&gt;Chisq)
## superModel2 6 9315.5 9356.2 -4651.8 9303.5
## superModel 7 9275.5 9323.0 -4630.8 9261.5 41.995 1 9.152e-11 ***
## ---
## Signif. codes: 0 '***' 0.001 '**' 0.01 '*' 0.05 '.' 0.1 ' ' 1</code></pre>
<p>Low Pr(&gt;Chisq) indicates that we can’t neglect component (1|Animal:Photo_ID_abs), therefore our ituition, that random component should be of the form Animal/Photo_ID_abs was correct.</p>
<p>We showed, that our model is reasonable. Now we can check, by permutational test, if our hypothesis was correct. By permuting column with interaction effect we see, that indeed there is significant interaction between mouse type and treatment.</p>
<pre class="r"><code>N &lt;- 1000
spines$interaction &lt;- (spines$mouse==&quot;WT&quot;)*(spines$treatment==&quot;li&quot;)
logs &lt;- replicate(N, logLik(lmer(log(length-0.03) ~ treatment + mouse + sample(interaction) + (1|Animal/Photo_ID_abs), data = spines)))
mean(logs &gt; logLik(superModel))</code></pre>
<pre><code>## [1] 0</code></pre>
</div>
<div id="conclusions" class="section level3">
<h3>4. Conclusions</h3>
<p>In study ‘ko’ both plots and computations show that there is an interaction between the mouse type and the treatment. Although the simplest model explaining length of spine doesn’t satisfy all assumptions of mixed models, by suitable transformation of data we can build a valid model, which shows the relation between length,treatment,mouse,animal and photo_ID_abs.</p>
</div>
